```
docdwarf@panix.com wrote:
> In article <9mulldFjihU1@mid.individual.net>,
> Pete Dashwood <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[31 more quoted lines elided]…
> get... dicey.

That smacks of "intellectual snobbery" in a computer context, Doc.  There 
are REAL and PERTINENT applications that DON'T run on mainframes. Fact of 
life. The machines which were once dismissed as "toy computers" started 
connecting to each other and  the synergy was more than anyone anticipated.

Like it or not, the Web is here to stay and there are some very serious 
transactions occurring across it every day. (Not to mention a lot of 
nonsense as well...) The fact is that the Internet is having far more effect 
on the general public than any mainframe system ever did. The Web in the 
cloud is more than capable of storing your decades of transactions and is 
doing so even as I write this.
>
> Architects design single-family houses, architects design multi-family
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> vacation cottages!'

Again, you are dismissing something because you think it is not on the scale 
you are working at.

But look again. Many of the mainframe systems which currently carry out the 
100 storey building stuff are being replaced by web based systems. Many of 
the 100 storey buildings are being demolished in favour of  10 storey 
buildings distributed to multiple locations, and these are found to be safer 
when the inevitable Cyber earthquakes strike the complex...

It is unwise to dismiss the future because it may not be running on a 
mainframe.
>
> I was taught - and, some might say, frequently demonstrate - that 'a
> little knowledge is a dangerous thing'.  Whether a weekly lesson by
> email in Javascript is more 'a little knowledge' might be for others
> to determine.

Well, this "other" believes that a "little knowledge" is just that, and it 
forms the basis for the acquisition of more knowledge. A weekly lesson by 
email in Javascript could be a very useful thing if you were trying to get 
to grips with Ajax or improve your users' experience of your web pages. 
Certainly, "a little knowledge" has to be better than NO knowledge... The 
aphorism you quoted is only true when the learning process stops and the 
person receiving the knowledge has delusions of grandeur. (If you know your 
limitations, you seek to either expand them by the acquisition of more 
knowledge, or you stay within what you know. There is no inherent danger in 
either of those options.)

My experience has been that ALL learning starts with "a little knowledge", 
so perhaps we regard this differently.
>
> [snip]
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> just do enough of it and *you* will find ways to do it better.

I would not and did not preclude any of the things you mention to enhance 
the learning experience. However, it is the nature of Human Intelligence 
that if we are allowed to repeat something often enough we will find better 
ways to do it.

I have a theory that smart people are lazy. (I'm not suggesting that lazy 
people are smart :-)).I can't prove this theory and I have never done a 
formal study of it (might be good material for a PhD Thesis...) but I 
believe it nevertheless. It is the desire to get something done with the 
minimum effort that often drives smart people to find better ways of doing 
it. Coding is no different. Every program you write you try to make better 
than the last one. After 30 years or so there is usually a significant 
difference between what you write now and what you wrote 30 years ago.(If 
there isn't, perhaps you'd be better suited to driving a truck?) No offence 
to truck drivers... it is just a different type of skill...


>
> [snip]
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> 'the logical process'.

But it is pure intellectual arrogance that suggests the general public don't 
have a slight command over 'the logical process'.

Certainly many of them won't feel that is where their interest lies, but 
they won't be the ones downloading Javascript. Of the ones who do, some will 
quit, deciding it is not for them. But there will be many (evidence suggests 
even millions) who will do it, like it, and be good at it.

You don't have to have a detailed knowledge of the dissertations of 
Aristotle, Chrysippus, Diodorus, or Philo, or know Boolean Algebra and 
Propositional Calculus in order to have command of "the logical process" 
(although all of these things may enhance your facility with it.)

It is called "Comon Sense" and it gets severely overlooked by the people who 
see logic as only formal, and measure capability against a piece of paper in 
a glass frame on the wall.

There is nothing "special" about ability to write COBOL (with the possible 
exception of a very small number of people like Bill Klein, Rick Smith et 
al, who really make it their life's work and deserve respect for doing 
so...). Writing Javascript is no more difficult than writing COBOL.

Once over a few beers, when I was much younger and less wise than I am now, 
I foolishly stated that I could take people off the street and in four weeks 
make them COBOL programmers. My Boss (who was older and wiser) gave me 4 
people who he said had come from no particular aptitude or background (they 
had actually come from different areas of the company but all had no 
previous experience with computers) and told me he wanted them trained by 
the end of the month.

They did me proud and 3 of them went on into programming careers. (The 
fourth I lost contact with so I don't know what happened to him.) Some years 
later in a different place and time I was tasked with training some existing 
COBOL people into OO COBOL. It was much more difficult and I never undertook 
it again, although I could probably do a better job of it now.

Let's not diminish the attempts of people who want to learn computer 
programming.

1. There is nothing "sacred" about it.
2. It CAN be learned.
3. The more comfortable people are with computer technology, the better 
place the world will be.

Pete.
```

```
that different people have different capabilities and leave the question of 
the intrinisc worth of Human Beings for another time.

Given that "different capabilities" is the case, you would accept that the 
"general public" will contain a set of people who do have "slight command 
over 'the logical process'."?

Then our argument comes down to the size of this set.

I contend it is the majority. I don't believe people are generally stupid, 
any more than they are generally evil. I don't believe that anyone gets out 
of bed in the morning and says: "I think I'll really screw my life up today. 
I'll destroy my workplace and alienate my friends and family. Probably try 
and get arrested before afternoon tea..."

And yet, clearly, these things do happen.

Summarising:

1. People generally are not stupid.
2. Computer progamming in Javascript is not difficult.
3. Many people, if given the opportunity, will learn to write Javascript.

The implicatons and observations following from this will be varied.

My take on it is that I have been wanting to see "computing for the masses" 
for more than 40 years. So any step in that direction makes me happy.

Certainly, the opportunity has been there for people to teach themselves for 
a very long time. I remember buying and programming a Sinclair ZX Spectrum 
and I was the proud owner of both a Commodore Pet and an Amiga (with PC 
bridgeboard). But I was also a professional programmer so it was natural I 
would indulge my work interests.

What we are seeing now is a sea change in the perception of computing. Where 
it was previously a dark, mysterious art, there is a growing realization 
(especially in the younger generation) that these things are no more 
difficult than doing a crossword puzzle or Sudoku, and they are certainly 
well within the capability of most of the population.

>
>>
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> ability?  Interesting possibility.

That is covered by the phrase "deciding it is not for them", but now that 
you mention it...

"Ability" is something that can be created.

It is largely predicated on motivation. If you want to do something badly 
enough you can develop the ability, provided, of course, that what you want 
to do is possible.

People are far more capable than they are led to believe. I am part of a 
music group here in Tauranga and in the course of it I get to work 
occasionally with people who are learning to play and sing. So often I find 
people who say: "I can't sing..." and they are right. And yet the majority 
of people have fine singing voices. They don't sing because at some time in 
their lives somebody said: "Who told you you could sing?" or "For Chrissake, 
shut it!". Get them over that and restore their confidence and suddenly they 
are joining the chorus. A very small percentage of people actually are tone 
deaf and cannot tell whether one sound is higher or lower than another. But 
that is a VERY small percentage.

If you start out really wanting to do something, have good tuition and a 
supportive group you will gradually come to the realization that you CAN do 
it. If you buy in to the idea that you are useless and incapable, all the 
tuition in the world will not achieve what you are attempting.

And success breeds success. As more and more goals are achieved confidence 
grows and so tougher goals can be set.

The majority of the general population are perfectly capable of writing 
their own web pages using free tools and Javascript. It doesn't take years 
of professional computer training.

>
>>
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> pertinent thing, Mr Dashwood.

I don't need to wait for you to suggest something , Doc. Neither do I need 
to agree that everything you suggest will be pertinent. Whatever I want to 
discuss is pertinent, because I want to discuss it. The above was not aimed 
at you it was a general disagreement with the idea that only people who have 
studied logic formally can be logical. Everythng I write here is not 
directly aimed at argument with you, although that part is fine too :-)
>
>>
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> common sense.

No, I don't think so. There is a vast difference between "Common Belief" 
(which includes "popular misconception") and Common Sense which is the 
ability of an individual to evaluate a given situation and behave rationally 
in it.
>
>>
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> itsself.

You seem to be labouring under the misguided belief that everything in my 
response is about you, what you said or what you didn't say.

Sorry, that is not the case. I'm not here to just argue with you, confined 
by the constraints of what you consider pertinent.

I make observations about things which arise from the discussion. I can 
respond to your post and introduce new ideas or observations at the same 
time because it is MY response.  They may or may not be predicated on what 
you said, were about to say, thought you might be going to say or did not in 
fact say or even think about saying.

Believe it or not but it isn't all about you (unless it is YOUR post of 
course; then it is... :-))

Pete.
```

```
*Think* of that construction, Mr Dashwood, and I am being a bit more

excluding the set of 'every'.

the sharpest'.  The latter allows for an equally-true 'every blade in this 
box is the dullest', among other logical amusements.
```

```
On 1/8/2012 5:03 PM, Pete Dashwood wrote: (snip)
>
> It is just possible that in my own lifetime I will see computing for the
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>

About 20 years ago I took a class on SAS, which if you haven't heard of 
it, SAS is an ad-hoc programming tool for end-users with lots of 
statistical analysis features.  I can't remember the instructor's name, 
but he told us that back in his days as a programmer he was told that 
soon there would be no need for assembler programmers like himself.  The 
end users would be able to write any programs they needed without any 
special training.  They would do it using a new programming language 
named COBOL.  Did that ever happen?

I think it's a good thing to give as many people as possible the 
experience of writing programs.  But like any human endeavor some people 
will be better at it than others.

If I can believe ZDNET and Confuserworld, PC's and Laptops are legacy 
systems, and smart phones are the programming platform of the future...

Kind regards,
```

```
> On 1/8/2012 5:03 PM, Pete Dashwood wrote: (snip)
>>

> Kind regards,

Good post, Steve.

I remember SAS...

I also remember "The Last One"  (never gonna be any more programming 
languages) and its cynical successor "The Next One"... :-)

I hate to think of the platforms and software I spent time learning, only to 
have them become obsolete within a few years.

But that is the nature of the beast and it wasn't all a write off.  We are 
each the sum total of our experiences and I don't believe there is any such 
thing as useless knowledge. (there may well be knowledge you haven't found a 
use for yet and there may be knowledge you think you no longer have need 
of... until the day you do.)

Personally, I believe the Von Neumann model will be superseded some time 
later this century and programming as we understand it will no longer be 
necessary. But in the meantime, it is fun and it keeps me off the streets... 
:-)

Pete.
```

```
I remember (it would have been in the mid-80s) being invited to a launch of 
"The Next One" in London. It may have been a hoax; I never went.

(The company I was working in had a good sense of humour and some of their 
internal memos were hilarious. My favourite was one from R & D where they 
claimed they had fabricated tachyon memory. Tachyons are supposed to move in 
negative time so memory fabricated from them would be able to give you an 
answer before you had asked the question...)

Since then we have come to the realization that one language is not going to 
cut it so the whole thing is just an amusing memory as far as I'm concerned.

Pete.
```

```
Blasted synapses... my error and apologies, my mistaken reference to JIR 
reinforced the timeline.  According to 
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thiotimoline the first article was published 
in 'Astounding Science Fiction' in March, 1948.

According to http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isaac_Asimov Dr Asimov earned 
his Phd in biochemistry from Columbia University in 1948.  From what 
little I know of the Groves of Academe I'd conclude it more likely than 
not that in those days a dissertation would not be defended so early in 
the semester.

Reading a bit further supports these assumptions.  The latter abovegiven 
URL yields the text 'In 1948 he also wrote a spoof chemistry article, "The 
Endochronic Properties of Resublimated Thiotimoline". At the time, Asimov 
was preparing his own doctoral dissertation, and for the oral examination 
to follow that.'

(following that is material further supporting Mr Brazee's assertion)

DD
```

```
On Tue, 10 Jan 2012 22:54:14 +0000 (UTC), docdwarf@panix.com () wrote:

>In article <9n3p0kFjs7U1@mid.individual.net>,
>Pete Dashwood <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
>DD

Your memory is not as porous as you may think.  Thiotimoline was a
fictitious chemical compound conceived by author Isaac Asimov and
first described in a spoof scientific paper titled "The Endochronic
Properties of Resublimated Thiotimoline" in 1948. Asimov went on to
write three additional short stories, each describing different
properties or uses of thiotimoline.

Regards,
```

```
On Jan 9, 12:03 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

> It is just possible that in my own lifetime I will see computing for the
> masses, with people taking control of their devices and without the
> mysterious veil that could only be penetrated by the priests of COBOL...:-)
> I have wanted this since 1965, so it is kind of pleasing to see things
> moving that way.

It seems that you were working in mainframe-land during the late 70s
through the 1980s and missed the Apple IIs, the ZX-80/81/Spectrums,
the BBCs, Amigas, various Ataris and the dozens of magazines and books
that supported them. My children's schools (and home) had some of
those and the pupils programmed them.

It was the 'priests of Windows' that closed that down. The schools
(well actually the Dept. of Education) were convinced (by Microsoft)
that Windows was the only future that was useful and that the students
should be trained (indoctrinated) to be Microsoft consumers and that
learning MS Word and Excel was the only path to future employment.

Fortunately this is changing back with phone and tablets leading the
way. It is easy to produce an iPhone or Android app, possibly too
easy. And with Arduino and (soon) Raspberry Pi this will expand into
controlling real equipment.
```

```
On Jan 11, 4:25 am, SkippyPB <swieg...@Nospam.neo.rr.com> wrote:
> On Tue, 10 Jan 2012 04:48:20 -0600, "HeyBub" <hey...@NOSPAMgmail.com>
> wrote:
…[38 more quoted lines elided]…
> poor management and vision doomed them.

You may note that the Amiga was not created by Commodore but by some
ex-Atari people who had formed Hi-Toro and later renamed the company
'Amiga'. Commodore bought the company and then added a bought in OS
called TripOS which had been developed at Cambridge University (UK).

TripOS was originally developed in BCPL*, the language that led to B
and then C.


* BCPL = Basic CPL (nothing to do with BASIC but the word is used in
the sense of fundemental), a cut down version of CPL = Combined
Programming Language that was being developed at the Atlas Laboratory
as a combination of several other languages (such as ACL = Atlas
Commercial Language).
```

<a id="t-c945b658e9"></a>
## Thread: Preprocessor directives — 21 msgs

### Preprocessor directives

- **From:** Sheky <shekhar07.nitdgp@gmail.com>
- **Date:** 2012-01-08T23:57:53-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<204986f2-40c5-4af8-9886-6221341b4e78@f1g2000yqi.googlegroups.com>`

```
Could anyone please answer why does cobol not support preprocessor
directives?
```

#### ↳ Re: Preprocessor directives

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2012-01-09T22:32:32+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9mvqhiF9q5U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<204986f2-40c5-4af8-9886-6221341b4e78@f1g2000yqi.googlegroups.com>`

```
Sheky wrote:
> Could anyone please answer why does cobol not support preprocessor
> directives?

Because a "Preprocessor" is NOT part of the language?

COBOL DOES support pre-processing for things like SQL and specialised file 
handling (to name but two; there are other possibilities... in fact, you 
could easily write your own COBOL pre-processor to do just about anything 
you wanted.)

You need to be more specific about WHICH "COBOL" you are referring to, and 
WHICH pre-processor you are having trouble with.

Pete
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Preprocessor directives

- **From:** Robert Wessel <robertwessel2@yahoo.com>
- **Date:** 2012-01-09T03:53:48-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<nfdlg791rgb06f9l7r59nda3rq8r6kehga@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<204986f2-40c5-4af8-9886-6221341b4e78@f1g2000yqi.googlegroups.com> <9mvqhiF9q5U1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On Mon, 9 Jan 2012 22:32:32 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

>Sheky wrote:
>> Could anyone please answer why does cobol not support preprocessor
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
>WHICH pre-processor you are having trouble with.


The forthcoming standard (Cobol-1x) includes what can only be
described as a preprocessor with the compiler-directing statements
(">>IF", and whatnot).  At least some of which has been implemented (I
think Microfocus is the model for much of the new stuff).
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Preprocessor directives

- **From:** "Bill Klein" <wmklein@noreply.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2012-01-10T15:16:17-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<GU1Pq.224488$tf7.38625@en-nntp-13.dc1.easynews.com>`
- **References:** `<204986f2-40c5-4af8-9886-6221341b4e78@f1g2000yqi.googlegroups.com> <9mvqhiF9q5U1@mid.individual.net> <nfdlg791rgb06f9l7r59nda3rq8r6kehga@4ax.com>`

```
Many compilers support "conditional" compilation (which was introduced in 
the 2002 Standard,not the next one).  See for example,
 Micro Focus
$IF, $EVATULATE statements

I haven't checked in detail, but I believe the Standard (2002) already 
supports:

>>IF  abe = "xyz"
>>source free
>>else
>>soruce fixed
>>endif

So, if what you are looking for is mixing conditional compilatin and other 
compiler dfirecting statements, that is already supported in "Standard" 
COBOL.

If you are looking for support for specifying options to "more typical" 
preprocessors (such as DB2, Oracle, or ReportWrioter), then you need to be 
specific about which compiler and which preprocessor you are talking about. 
As far as I know, all of the "supported" comibinations of compilers and 
preprocessors allow for specificaiton of preprocessor "directives" with 
integrated compilers.

"Robert Wessel" <robertwessel2@yahoo.com> wrote in message 
news:nfdlg791rgb06f9l7r59nda3rq8r6kehga@4ax.com...

> think Microfocus is the model for much of the new stuff).
```

```
On Jan 9, 8:57 pm, Sheky <shekhar07.nit...@gmail.com> wrote:
> Could anyone please answer why does cobol not support preprocessor
> directives?

There is nothing to prevent you (except perhaps restrictive site
standards) from writing your own preprocessor for specific needs.

I have done this in the past. One was a Python program that converted
between MF and Fujitsu variations by recognising 'directives' in
columns 1-6 and adding or removing an asterisk in column 7 based on
parameters for the run.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Preprocessor directives

- **From:** SkippyPB <swiegand@Nospam.neo.rr.com>
- **Date:** 2012-01-11T10:32:59-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<ejarg7pss9msbds1fv9fm59vtp7dkgudcj@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<204986f2-40c5-4af8-9886-6221341b4e78@f1g2000yqi.googlegroups.com> <c3ee0479-3e8d-4c7a-b1f7-eeadd05de1a5@f11g2000yql.googlegroups.com>`

```
On Tue, 10 Jan 2012 13:38:39 -0800 (PST), Richard
<riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:

>On Jan 9, 8:57ï¿½pm, Sheky <shekhar07.nit...@gmail.com> wrote:
>> Could anyone please answer why does cobol not support preprocessor
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>parameters for the run.

My employer wrote a pre-processor for COBOL many years ago and it is
still in use today.  We had developed our own language to produce
reports and other things that was supposed to make it possible for
non-programmers to program.  It didn't work out that way, but the
system is useful for quick programs used in batch, online or both.  It
has its own commands which the preprocessor translates into COBOL
statements.

The preprocessor is written in Assembler and employees some C code via
a call.  Personally I don't like it and only use it when forced to by
circumstance.  Other programmers love it.

But the point is the pre-processor reads the source, translates the
"special" code into COBOL statements, creates an output file of new
source which is then read either into the CICS preprocessor or the
COBOL preprocessor.

Regards,
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Preprocessor directives

_(thread depth: 6)_

- **From:** Robert Wessel <robertwessel2@yahoo.com>
- **Date:** 2012-01-12T06:24:14-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<7ojtg7hem1lbrinhndjvereosrggpbldsv@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<9n61vqF39jU1@mid.individual.net> <1f596384acbc592246b50a5d531b4e2c@dizum.com> <9n804gF63sU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On 12 Jan 2012 11:57:04 GMT, billg999@cs.uofs.edu (Bill Gunshannon)
wrote:

>In article <1f596384acbc592246b50a5d531b4e2c@dizum.com>,
>	Nomen Nescio <nobody@dizum.com> writes:
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>with nroff, just as well.


Preprocessors can have a varying amount of integration with the
languages they support.  C's, for example, largely assumes that the
input can be sensibly tokenized by the normal C rules.  That can (for
example) make a hash out of certain PICTURE clauses, if you're feeding
it a Cobol program.  Other preprocessors are much more generic in
their text processing (m4, for example).  Although I don't think you
can really call either "the Unix preprocessor".

Of course the very wide availability of C's preprocessor has meant
that it's been (ab)used for all sorts of purposes.
```

```
to the best of my knowledge, IBM (at least the z/OS sided) does NOT have a 
"single" preprocessor for COBOL.  It does have an "integrated coprocessor" 
which hancdles what the previous CICS and DB2 preprocessord handled.

It also has the EXIT compiler option which allows a user to wrigte a 
"pre-/post-proccesor" - which can be writen in COBOL and which can handle 
any input source code, copybooks, or listing output.
```

## Thread: Detecting cursor control keys — 6 msgs

_Topics: [`Databases and SQL`](topics.md#databases)_

### Detecting cursor control keys

- **From:** razor <iruddock@blueyonder.co.uk>
- **Date:** 2012-01-10T02:36:19-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<c45fe054-3401-4243-9e6a-67933740b866@z1g2000vbx.googlegroups.com>`

```
Hi All

I'm using MF Cobol (NE5.1) and unfortunately we only use screen
section (no GUI). I want to be able to detect when one of the cursor
keys has been pressed (up,down,left, right). I currently use the X"B0"
keyboard routine to detect other function keys, page up/down, etc, but
as far as I can see I can't include these. In most cases this is
correct but I have a small 'date picker' program that it would suit
more to be able to 'detect' these 4 keys.

Anybody got any ideas?

Thanks chaps

Ian
```

#### ↳ Re: Detecting cursor control keys

- **From:** Alistair Maclean <alistair.j.l.maclean@gmail.com>
- **Date:** 2012-01-10T07:37:04-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<99073fd3-870a-4367-bd47-be2f964e017f@t13g2000vbt.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<c45fe054-3401-4243-9e6a-67933740b866@z1g2000vbx.googlegroups.com>`

```
On Jan 10, 10:36 am, razor <irudd...@blueyonder.co.uk> wrote:
> Hi All
>
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> Ian

This looks too easy to answer but Chapter 19 Key Code List (search for
cursor keys in the help) of MF Cobol's help provides:

Mnemonic  Function
ESC       ESCape
F1 – F12  Function keys 1 – 12
CR        Carriage Return
DEL       DELete
CURL      CURsor Left
CURR      CURsor Right
CURU      CURsor Up
CURD      CURsor Down

so I must be barking up the wrong tree (probably the one that has all
of the management monkeys in it).
```

- **References:** `<c45fe054-3401-4243-9e6a-67933740b866@z1g2000vbx.googlegroups.com> <99073fd3-870a-4367-bd47-be2f964e017f@t13g2000vbt.googlegroups.com>`

#### ↳ Re: Detecting cursor control keys

- **From:** Richard <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2012-01-10T10:54:33-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<ac57ac5f-c7f6-4c34-900b-1e4860d797b5@y12g2000yqc.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<c45fe054-3401-4243-9e6a-67933740b866@z1g2000vbx.googlegroups.com>`

```
On Jan 10, 11:36 pm, razor <irudd...@blueyonder.co.uk> wrote:
> Hi All
>
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> more to be able to 'detect' these 4 keys.

The x"B0" routine is described in the manual as machine dependent and
creates conflicts with other features. I would have thought that the
best ADIS mechanism is to use the Special-Names clause "CRT STATUS IS
Key-Status" and the various function key values are put into the data
area Key-Status.

The User function keys need enabling with CALL x"AF" if they are
outside the default range of Esc, F1 to F10.

The arrow keys are normally configured as 'adis keys' which are used
during and accept (ie don't terminate it) but this can be changed
using a CALL x"AF" to make them function keys and these can be changed
back with another CALL.

There should also be an adiscf (or adiscfw for Windows) which can do
all this configuration into the ADISCTRL configuration file. However,
as you probably want to change the usage only temporarily you would
need to use the CALL x"AF".

See ADIS section in your System Reference.
```

#### ↳ Re: Detecting cursor control keys

- **From:** Alain Reymond <arwebmail@skynet.be>
- **Date:** 2012-01-11T09:11:19+01:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<4f0d4445$0$5036$ba620e4c@news.skynet.be>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<c45fe054-3401-4243-9e6a-67933740b866@z1g2000vbx.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<c45fe054-3401-4243-9e6a-67933740b866@z1g2000vbx.googlegroups.com>`

```
Le 10/01/2012 11:36, razor a ï¿½crit :
> Hi All
>
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> Ian

Ian,

I see no other solution than using x'AF'.
A small example :

       special-names.
           crt status is crt-93-etat.
 ...
       78 CrUp    value 5.
       78 CrDn    value 6.
       01  crt-93-etat pic 999.
       01  crt-etat redefines crt-93-etat.
           02 key-1                           pic 9.
           02 key-2                           pic 99 comp.
           02 key-3                           pic 99 comp.
       *>Parameters for ADIS.
       01 Set-Bit-Pairs        pic 99 comp-x value 1.
       01 Parameter-Block.
           02  Bit-Pair-Setting    pic 99 comp-x.
           02  filler              pic x  value "2".
           02  Bit-Pair-Number     pic 99 comp-x.
           02  Bit-Key-Number      pic 99 comp-x value 1.
....
      screen section.
       01 ecran01.
 ...
      procedure division.
...
          move 0 to wss-i2
           move 3 to Bit-Pair-Setting
           move 27 to Bit-Pair-Number
           move 2 to Bit-Key-Number
           call x"af" using set-bit-pairs Parameter-Block
           *> enable crup et crdn
           move 1 to Bit-Pair-Setting
           move 5 to Bit-Pair-Number
           move 2 to Bit-Key-Number
           call x"af" using set-bit-pairs Parameter-Block

              display ecran01
               accept ecran01
               end-accept
               evaluate true
                 when key-1 = 0
...
                 when key-1 = 1
...
                 when key-1 = 2  *> cursor up dn
                   evaluate key-2
                       when CrDn
...
                        when CrUp
...
                   end-evaluate
               end-evaluate
...
           *> disable crup et crdn
           move 2 to Bit-Pair-Setting.
           move 5 to Bit-Pair-Number.
           move 2 to Bit-Key-Number.
           call x"af" using set-bit-pairs Parameter-Block
...

Good luck!
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Detecting cursor control keys

- **From:** razor <iruddock@blueyonder.co.uk>

## Thread: Cobol is On The Rise?!? — 18 msgs

### Cobol is On The Rise?!?

- **From:** iNFO_rene <infodynamics_ph@yahoo.com>
- **Date:** 2012-01-18T20:58:39-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<ae2e4eb9-58b7-48aa-ace7-06e7a8049410@q7g2000pbg.googlegroups.com>`

```
2012! New year for Cobol. October 2010 column really makes me wonder
how this language persist itself since then. For me, the reason behind
this is that Cobol is English-like straight command language that is
easy to learn and even more to re-use codes. Retraining new
programmers is easy. The column maintains Cobol at number 7... in line
with Python and Ruby.

http://www.infoworld.com/d/developer-world/7-programming-languages-the-rise-620?page=0,3&source=rs

In today's "online solutions" (or cloud computing) I still rely in
Cobol for my HTML pages. Well, Microsoft Silverlight project withdraws
from the pack... who knows Microsoft will invent another language in 2
or 3 years from now gearing towards "real" web scenarios. Then maybe
everyone would jump into it again.
```

#### ↳ Re: Cobol is On The Rise?!?

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2012-01-20T00:59:04+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9nqesaFp09U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<ae2e4eb9-58b7-48aa-ace7-06e7a8049410@q7g2000pbg.googlegroups.com>`

```
iNFO_rene wrote:
> 2012! New year for Cobol. October 2010 column really makes me wonder
> how this language persist itself since then. For me, the reason behind
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> with Python and Ruby.

That is an ambiguous (and misleading) statement.

COBOL is not in the Top Twenty programming languages, although it has shown 
some resurgence from 30th. The article is about SOME computer languages 
which are showing some resurgence. COBOL is one of these.


> http://www.infoworld.com/d/developer-world/7-programming-languages-the-rise-620?page=0,3&source=rs

In my opinion this is a poor article. (Nothing to do with it mentioning 
COBOL but because of the incorrect and misleading statements in it about 
other languages.)

Did you read the 4 comments posted on it? I find myself in complete 
agreement with Matt Green.

>
> In today's "online solutions" (or cloud computing) I still rely in
> Cobol for my HTML pages.

I used COBOL CGI and ISAPI code for some web sites and found it good. 
However, it doesn't have the flexibility of ASP.Net.

>Well, Microsoft Silverlight project withdraws
> from the pack... who knows Microsoft will invent another language in 2
> or 3 years from now gearing towards "real" web scenarios. Then maybe
> everyone would jump into it again.

Silverlight is no more "withdrawn from the pack" than Adobe Flash is. 
Official support for both of them has been withdrawn but thousands of 
developers will continue using them and existing sites which use these 
facilities are unlikely to get changed any time soon.

(The COBOL21 site linked to this forum uses Flash for the sunrise animation, 
but all of the demo videos for the PRIMA Toolset, accessible from the 
COBOL21 and PRIMA computing websites, use HTML5, with "fallback" to Flash if 
the accessing Browser has no HTML5 support.
http://primacomputing.co.nz/cobol21
http://primacomputing.co.nz/COBOL21/demosandtutorials.aspx
Both of these sites are ASP.Net sites using C# code-behinds and shared 
Master pages.)

Silverlight is being replaced by  HTML5/CSS and XAML (the basis of the new 
Metro interface) which is actually even better than Silverlight in some 
regards. I have done some test Silverlight projects using Microsoft 
Expression Blend for the development and found it excellent. The same tool 
can be used for Windows 8 development and I'm trying to do some of that when 
I get time. (I don't have a lot currently). Silverlight projects will be 
easily convertible to Windows 8 and it can be done automatically by the 
development Toolset. I don't know what the path is for Flash, although much 
of what is currently achievable with Flash is easily achievable with HTML5.

The "preferred path" for Web development (for Microsoft developers) will be 
Metro which is based around XAML and Javascript is just one of the languages 
that can be used to implement the Metro interface. (Although I have nothing 
against Javascript, I'll probably continue using C# for web development. (VS 
2011 can generate XAML for Metro directly from C# apparently, although I 
have yet to see this...))

Microsoft have basically bet the farm on Metro and if the public don't like 
it, their $70 billion a year business could be cut to a mere $30 
billion...:-) If the public DO like it, they will be assured of continued 
superiority for the next 10 years...

Indications so far are that it is being well received and has already won at 
least one prestigious award for innovative design.
(http://www.winrumors.com/windows-phone-wins-idea-2011-peoples-choice-design-award/)

It will see the end of Windows as we understand them and a much deeper 
"personalization" of both mobile and static devices. It will also usher in a 
time when your workplace computer will have the same interface as your 
phone, your tablet and your XBox.

Despite the topic of this thread, it is unlikely that COBOL will figure in 
any of it.

Pete.
```

```
The term "lowest common denominator" comes to mind. Specifically,
I considered the card game, Solitaire. I can play the game on my
desktop using a mouse, on my laptop using its track pad, and
on my TV using its remote control. (Also, on a table with a real
deck of cards, but that's tangential. <g>) Now, were there a
common "interface" for playing Solitaire, It would be limited to
arrow keys to move cards, a button to select the cards to move,
and buttons to "play again" and "exit". It seems more reasonable
to use that which works well for each "platform", rather than
that which is common to all.

OTOH, maybe I don't understand what "Metro" is supposed to be
and don't care enough to find out. <g>

-----
In other matters, I got the laptop and, finally, a cable modem
Thursday. Friday evening I got the latest Windows updates for
Windows 7. After the computer went through all it rebooting
and such, I was checking out what software was installed when
the computer "locked up" with the mouse pointer over a start
menu item. So, within five minutes of installing the updates,
I had to find out how to remove the battery. I am not a fan
of Microsoft. <g>

Accordingly, the desktop will be upgraded to Linux in a few
weeks. <g>
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ OT: portable versus external hard drives for backup

_(thread depth: 4)_

- **From:** Clark F Morris <cfmpublic@ns.sympatico.ca>
- **Date:** 2012-01-20T13:40:59-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<0o9jh7d1t9utquu4908akitbn4hs20cetc@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<ae2e4eb9-58b7-48aa-ace7-06e7a8049410@q7g2000pbg.googlegroups.com> <9nqesaFp09U1@mid.individual.net> <e941f114-52b7-4070-a849-b2fc042e7be0@kn4g2000pbc.googlegroups.com> <9nt6mbFodaU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
I have an external 2 terabyte hard drive that I use as a back up
device by plugging it into each of the two computers I have and then
returning it to a fire safe.  I am planning to get a second drive so I
can rotate the backups off-site and was wondering if the portable hard
drives are worth the additional price for this usage.  I am assuming
that better shock protection is given the hard drive in the portable
versions.

Clark Morris
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: OT: portable versus external hard drives for backup

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: OT: portable versus external hard drives for backup

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** Robert Wessel <robertwessel2@yahoo.com>
- **Date:** 2012-01-20T16:24:09-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<hgpjh7h09951na73rbveb6vtm7av63gr6t@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<ae2e4eb9-58b7-48aa-ace7-06e7a8049410@q7g2000pbg.googlegroups.com> <9nqesaFp09U1@mid.individual.net> <e941f114-52b7-4070-a849-b2fc042e7be0@kn4g2000pbc.googlegroups.com> <9nt6mbFodaU1@mid.individual.net> <0o9jh7d1t9utquu4908akitbn4hs20cetc@4ax.com>`

```
On Fri, 20 Jan 2012 13:40:59 -0400, Clark F Morris
<cfmpublic@ns.sympatico.ca> wrote:

>I have an external 2 terabyte hard drive that I use as a back up
>device by plugging it into each of the two computers I have and then
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>versions.


While laptop drives have substantially higher *operating* shock
tolerance than desktop drives, the non-operating tolerances are
usually only a little higher.

For example, the (desktop) Seagate Barracuda's have operating shock
limits of 63G for 2ms, and non-operating limits of 300G for 2ms.

The (laptop) Seagate Momentus have operating limits of 350G for 2ms,
and non-operating of 1000G for 1ms.

So long as they're powered off and in a bit of padding, you're not
going to see much difference in their ability to survive transport or
shipping.

Laptop drives have the advantage of being much lower power, so you
usually don't need a power brick with a laptop-drive based external
hard drive, and are physically smaller and lighter, but are
substantially lower in capacity, substantially slower, and
substantially more expensive.

IOW, use the cheaper and faster desktop-drive based for backup, unless
you really need the portability of the smaller drives, or need to
actually use them in a mobile situation.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol is On The Rise?!?

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2012-01-21T04:05:11+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9nte5aFo24U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<ae2e4eb9-58b7-48aa-ace7-06e7a8049410@q7g2000pbg.googlegroups.com> <9nqesaFp09U1@mid.individual.net> <e941f114-52b7-4070-a849-b2fc042e7be0@kn4g2000pbc.googlegroups.com>`

```
iNFO_rene wrote:
>> That is an ambiguous (and misleading) statement.
>>
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
> its not worth it.

I don't think it is quite as black and white as that, Rene.

What has changed is that it is much more difficult nowadays to get by with 
just one language. For many years COBOL was the only game in town for 
commercial data processing and it is still very good for that.  It was quite 
enough to know COBOL and maybe a bit of Assembler for the platform you were 
working with, and that was about it really.

The technology was centralised and there was separation between most people 
and the computers that processed business. It was all mysterious and arcane 
for the average man in the street, who had no idea about how these machines 
worked or what they could do.

What changed was that the technology became so advanced that it was possible 
to put computing power onto the desktops where it was needed so that 
branches of the Business had immediate access to data instead of having to 
wait for the overnight batch to produce a listing and look at it tomorrow. 
People started connecting up the toy computers and discovered a synergy that 
was almost unimaginable. New ways of doing business started to emerge and 
they were not constricted by having a central processing point. At the same 
time advances were made in software technology and new paradigms emerged 
which were ideal for the idea of networked computer power. "Computer 
Science" evolved and spilled over into Business. Communications took off and 
it was expected that remote branches of the Business could be contacted and 
connected to without it costing an arm and a leg.

Although some very alert and progressive vendors added the new facilities to 
COBOL the community at large rejected it because it wasn't what they were 
used to and they simply didn't get why it was important. COBOL got left 
behind. Even those of us who did see the importance of things like Classes 
and objects soon came to the realization that using the COBOL version of 
these things was way less productive than using the same facilities in other 
languages. The final nail in the coffin was that COBOL became increasingily 
expensive and everythig else got cheaper.

That doesn't mean it is worthless, but it does mean that there are other 
languages which are better for modern development.

It isn't about emotional attachment to a Legacy language, it is about the 
most cost-effective tools available. These days it isn't enough to be a "one 
trick pony". You need skills with languages and scripts for the Web, for the 
computer, and probably soon for the pocket devices which will become the 
computer wave of tomorrow. For some of these things COBOL can be used, but 
it is far from the best tool for the job.

COBOL is experiencing an upsurge in demand because the "knowledge base " for 
it is dying off or retiring and it is not generally being taught.Although 
there is still need for people to maintain their legacy while they look at 
what to do next, there are very few development shops that see COBOL as 
being where their IT future is. However, it is a market place and like all 
markets, the laws of supply and demand drive the costs. As the rates go up 
people become interested in the language and some of them decide to learn 
it. (I have two people currently who are in contact with me because they 
want to learn COBOL and it isn't on the curriculum at their respective seats 
of learning. These are bright people who are proactive about their 
education. One of them is planning making a niche business in modernising 
COBOL, or migrating people off it. He seems to think that the market for 
this is increasing (I hope he's right, but things are pretty quiet at the 
moment... :-))

Still, past experience has shown me that sometimes very interesting 
challenges drop suddenly out of the Blue so I'm still hanging in there at 
the moment.

I think when it comes to COBOL it really isn't too important what the 
general consensus is. (It would be if you were a young person seeking to 
start a career and needing to be employed...) but if you are programming on 
your own account you can pretty much use whatever you feel comfortable with. 
If COBOL works for you (and you can afford it), use COBOL. If you are 
concerned about overall cost of ownership you might want to look at 
something else.

As I said earlier, it isn't just black and white, there are shades of grey 
in there.

Pete.
```

```
ASP.NET (code behind) don't care about HTML5/CSS3 formatting...
because it creates its own form. Html-based codes can be used outside
of it though because it is still a regular webpage. HTML5 is still new
and most of the formatting options they provide has browser
incompatibilities yet, though I am pretty sure the culture will grow
(IF they persist) in a couple of years.

On the other side, HTML5 development does not include ASP.NET as an
alternative either. These are the groups who separate themselves from
Microsoft tech as they conceived their association to be an "open
source" advocates (aah, facebook generation). BUT looking at the
possibility of combining these two technologies, THAT is gold mine.
I'm looking at how these two players are going. One who persist wins!
```

```
"iNFO_rene" <infodynamics_ph@yahoo.com> wrote in message 
news:f60e3489-de4a-44cd-bf0d-dbd1356a2c4b@3g2000pbd.googlegroups.com...
>ASP.NET (code behind) don't care about HTML5/CSS3 formatting...
>because it creates its own form.

Yes, but that is just a skeleton. You can override it. You are right that 
the code-behind doesn't care about the form definition (HTML) and that is 
the whole point of it. It is to separate the presentation FORMAT (HTML/CSS) 
from the presentation CONTENT (manipulated and server up by the code-behind)

>Html-based codes can be used outside
>of it though because it is still a regular webpage.
Exactly. :-)



>HTML5 is still new
>and most of the formatting options they provide has browser
>incompatibilities yet, though I am pretty sure the culture will grow
>(IF they persist) in a couple of years.

I guess it depends on how you define "new"... I first coded using it a 
couple of years ago... Personally, I am not so concerned with the formatting 
aspects of the tags (because that is a perennial problem with whatever level 
of HTML you implement), but rather with the NEW (NOT in HTML4) tags it 
provides (especially video and canvas type tags.)

>On the other side, HTML5 development does not include ASP.NET as an
>alternative either.

Because they are not alternative technologies. They are complementary.You 
can use ANY level of HTML with ASP.NET.

I see HTML5/ASP.NET as a stepping stone. At the moment I provide C# 
code-behinds for ASP.NET, which I develop with Visual Studio. Later, 
probably within the next two years, I'll simply let the C# compiler generate 
XAML (Visual Studio 2011 already supports this apparently), use MS Blend for 
the form design in HTML5, (or possibly the VS web design tools if they have 
improved ot the level of Blend by then) and bypass ASP.NET altogether.

> These are the groups who separate themselves from
>Microsoft tech as they conceived their association to be an "open
>source" advocates (aah, facebook generation). BUT looking at the
>possibility of combining these two technologies, THAT is gold mine.
>I'm looking at how these two players are going. One who persist wins!

I think you'll find ASP.Net will be superseded by the process I just 
described. It is a natural evolution.

>
> For current web development I use HTML5 (with C# code-behinds in ASP.NET)
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Pete.
```

## Thread: How to Earn $2,500 every month without Investment through part time jobs? — 1 msgs

_Topics: [`Jobs, careers, recruiting, salary`](topics.md#jobs) · [`Help requests and how-to`](topics.md#help)_

### How to Earn $2,500 every month without Investment through part time jobs?

- **From:** "businessmother@hotmail.com" <businessmother@hotmail.com>
- **Date:** 2012-01-21T13:59:36-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<ff2c076a-729e-4130-8d5e-227d6c19e0fe@o9g2000yqa.googlegroups.com>`

```
How to Earn $2,500 every month without Investment through part time
jobs?

You can earn $2,500 the very first month from data entry jobs on
internet. This is an easy work from home form filling job. Work less
than 1 hr daily. No investment. Open for anyone, worldwide. Please
visit the website http://www.earnparttimejobs.com/index.php?id=3677959
```

## Thread: Classic ASP autocomplete (using Ajax/Cobol COM+ wrapper) — 3 msgs

### Classic ASP autocomplete (using Ajax/Cobol COM+ wrapper)

- **From:** iNFO_rene <infodynamics_ph@yahoo.com>
- **Date:** 2012-01-24T21:11:26-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<c0ad7931-02c8-4cc1-af27-8d858d2a650d@q7g2000pbg.googlegroups.com>`

```
Just wanted to post my usual classic ASP autocomplete using Ajax
(coupled with Cobol COM wrapper/dll). It composed of three (3) files
as follows;
ajaxPage3.ASP
ajaxPage3a.JS
ajaxPage3a.ASP

file number 1: ajaxPage3.ASP

<%@language="vbscript" codepage="1252"%>
<?xml version="1.0" encoding="UTF-8"?>
<!DOCTYPE html PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD XHTML 1.0 Transitional//EN" "http://
www.w3.org/TR/xhtml1/DTD/xhtml1-transitional.dtd">
<%
Dim qry, iBrand
iBrand = ""
qry = trim(request.Querystring("qFunc"))
if qry = "jxBrand" then
  iBrand = trim(request.Querystring("qBrand"))
end if
%>
<html xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml">
<head><title>Sample Autocomplete AJAX using Classic ASP</title>
<meta http-equiv="content-type" content="text/html; charset=us-ascii" /
>
<meta name="author" content="Rene Aquino Surop" />
<script type="text/javascript" src="ajaxPage3a.js"></script>
</head>
<body>
<form name="aspForm" action="ajaxPage3.asp" method="post" />
<div style="margin-top:15px; border:1px solid #3366ff; padding:10px;">
	<label for="jBrand">Car Brand: </label><div id="brandDiv"
style="position:absolute; z-index:100;" /></div>&nbsp;
	<input id="txtBrand" name="txtBrand" value="<%=trim(iBrand)%>"
onKeyUp="srchBrand(this.value)" />
</div>
</form>
<div style="margin-top:15px; border:1px solid #3366ff; padding:10px;">
  The sample Autocomplete provides suggestions while you type into the
field. Here the suggestions are brand names, displayed when a
character are entered into the field.<br /><br />
  The datasource is a classic ASP server-side script which returns
HTML data, specified via a simple URL for the source-option.
</div>
</body>
</html>


file number 2: ajaxPage3a.JS (javascript)


/* car brand searching script  */
var a;
function srchBrand(varTxt){
	if(varTxt.length>0){
		var b="ajaxPage3a.asp?sid="+Math.random()
+"&qAction=Search&qBrand="+varTxt;
		a=GetXmlHttpObject(recResult);
		document.getElementById("brandDiv").style.display='';
		document.getElementById("brandDiv").innerHTML="<img src='/images/
arrow.gif'/>";
		a.open("GET",b,true);a.send(null);}
	else{
		document.getElementById("brandDiv").style.display='none';
		document.getElementById("brandDiv").innerHTML="";}}

function recResult(){
	if(a.readyState==4||a.readyState=="complete"){
		document.getElementById("brandDiv").innerHTML=a.responseText;}}

/* asynchronous javascript object */
function GetXmlHttpObject(handler){
	var d=null;
	if(navigator.userAgent.indexOf("MSIE")>=0){
		var e="Msxml2.XMLHTTP";
		if(navigator.appVersion.indexOf("MSIE 5.5")>=0){
			e="Microsoft.XMLHTTP";}
		try{
			d=new ActiveXObject(e);
			d.onreadystatechange=handler;
			return d;}
		catch(e){
			alert("Browser Error. Unable to perform AJAX feature");
			return;}}

	if(navigator.userAgent.indexOf("Mozilla")>=0 ||
navigator.userAgent.indexOf("Opera")>=0){
		d=new XMLHttpRequest();
		d.onload=handler;
		d.onerror=handler;
		return d;}}


file number 3: ajaxPage3a.ASP


<%
Dim action, iBrand, iResult
action = trim(request.QueryString("qAction"))
if action = "Search" then
  iBrand = trim(request.QueryString("qBrand"))
	'this part is actually a server-side Cobol COM wrapper or object that
is fetching data as below;
	'DUMMY RESULT ONLY
	iResult = "<div style='border:1px #ccc solid; margin-top:23px; margin-
left:5px; width:200px;'><table width='100%' cellpadding='0px'
cellspacing='0px' align='center'>"
	iResult = iResult + "<tr bgcolor='#cccc99'><td style='width:100px'><a
href='ajaxPage3.asp?qFunc=jxBrand&qBrand=Chevrolet'>Chevrolet</a></
td></tr>"
	iResult = iResult + "<tr bgcolor='#ffffff'><td style='width:100px'><a
href='ajaxPage3.asp?qFunc=jxBrand&qBrand=Ford'>Ford</a></td></tr>"
	iResult = iResult + "<tr bgcolor='#cccc99'><td style='width:100px'><a
href='ajaxPage3.asp?qFunc=jxBrand&qBrand=Honda'>Honda</a></td></tr>"
	iResult = iResult + "<tr bgcolor='#ffffff'><td style='width:100px'><a
href='ajaxPage3.asp?qFunc=jxBrand&qBrand=Hyundai'>Hyundai</a></td></
tr>"
	iResult = iResult + "<tr bgcolor='#cccc99'><td style='width:100px'><a
href='ajaxPage3.asp?qFunc=jxBrand&qBrand=Toyota'>Toyota</a></td></tr>"
	iResult = iResult + "</table></div>"

	response.write(iResult)
end if
%>


That's about it. If anyone could improve it, then it is better. Today
though, jQuery is a good alternative as well.
```

## Thread: CONCEPT OF GOD IN ISLAM !!!!!!!!!!!!!!! — 1 msgs

### CONCEPT OF GOD IN ISLAM !!!!!!!!!!!!!!!

- **From:** BV BV <bv5bv5bv5@yahoo.com>
- **Date:** 2012-01-25T04:54:31-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<ecd5f4e9-d3fc-4026-8a80-12a98f2cf8a6@k28g2000yqc.googlegroups.com>`

```
CONCEPT OF GOD IN ISLAM

Sorry for not sending anything related to this group but it might be
something new to you.
CONCEPT OF GOD IN ISLAM
It is a known fact that every language has one or more terms that are
used in reference to God and sometimes to lesser deities. This is not
the case with Allah. Allah is the personal name of the One true God.
Nothing else can be called Allah. The term has no plural or gender.
This shows its uniqueness when compared with the word god which can be
made plural, gods, or feminine, goddess. It is interesting to notice
that Allah is the personal name of God in Aramaic, the language of
Jesus and a sister language of Arabic. The One true God is a
reflection of the unique concept that Islam associates with God. To a
Muslim, Allah is the Almighty, Creator and Sustainer of the universe,
Who is similar to nothing and nothing is comparable to Him. The
Prophet Muhammad was asked by his contemporaries about Allah; the
answer came directly from God Himself in the form of a short chapter
of the Quran, which is considered the essence of the unity or the
motto of monotheism. This is chapter 112 which reads:

"In the name of God, the Merciful, the Compassionate. Say (O Muhammad)
He is God the One God, the Everlasting Refuge, who has not begotten,
nor has been begotten, and equal to Him is not anyone."

Some non Muslims allege that God in Islam is a stern and cruel God who
demands to be obeyed fully. He is not loving and kind. Nothing can be
farther from truth than this allegation. It is enough to know that,
with the exception of one, each of the 114 chapters of the Quran
begins with the verse: "In the name of God, the Merciful, the
Compassionate." In one of the sayings of Prophet Muhammad (PBUH) we
are told that "God is more loving and kinder than a mother to her dear
child." But God is also Just. Hence evildoers and sinners must have
their share of punishment and the virtuous, His bounties and favors.
Actually God’s attribute of Mercy has full manifestation in His
attribute of Justice. People suffering throughout their lives for His
sake and people oppressing and exploiting other people all their lives
should not receive similar treatment from their Lord. Expecting
similar treatment for them will amount to negating the very belief in
the accountability of man in the Hereafter and thereby negating all
the incentives for a moral and virtuous life in this world. The
following Quranic verses are very clear and straightforward in this
respect:

"Verily, for the Righteous are gardens of Delight, in the Presence of
their Lord. Shall We then treat the people of Faith like the people of
Sin? What is the matter with you? How judge you?" (68:34-36)


Islam rejects characterizing God in any human form or depicting Him as
favoring certain individuals or nations on the basis of wealth, power
or race. He created the human beings as equals. They may distinguish
themselves and get His favor through virtue and piety only. The
concept that God rested in the seventh day of creation, that God
wrestled with one of His soldiers, that God is an envious plotter
against mankind, or that God is incarnate in any human being are
considered blasphemy from the Islamic point of view. The unique usage
of Allah as a personal name of God is a reflection of Islam’s emphasis
on the purity of the belief in God which is the essence of the message
of all God’s messengers. Because of this, Islam considers associating
any deity or personality with God as a deadly sin which God will never
forgive, despite the fact He may forgive all other sins.
The Creator must be of a different nature from the things created
because if he is of the same nature as they are, he will be temporal
and will therefore need a maker. It follows that nothing is like Him.
If the maker is not temporal, then he must be eternal. But if he is
eternal, he cannot be caused, and if nothing outside him causes him to
continue to exist, which means that he must be Self-Sufficient. And if
He does not depend on anything for the continuance of His own
existence, then this existence can have no end. The Creator is
therefore eternal and everlasting: ‘He is the First and the Last.’ He
is Self-Sufficient or Self-Subsistent or, to use a Quranic term, Al-
Qayyum. The Creator does not create only in the sense of bringing
things into being, He also preserves them and takes them out of
existence and is the ultimate cause of whatever happens to them.
"God is the Creator of everything. He is the guardian over everything.
Unto Him belong the keys of the heavens and the earth." (39:62, 63)
"No creature is there crawling on the earth, but its provision rests
on God. He knows its lodging place and its repository." (11:6)


GOD’S ATTRIBUTES
If the Creator is Eternal and Everlasting, then His attributes must
also be eternal and everlasting. He should not lose any of His
attributes nor acquire new ones. If this is so, then His attributes
are absolute. Can there be more than one Creator with such absolute
attributes? Can there be for example, two absolutely powerful
Creators? A moment’s thought shows that this is not feasible. The
Quran summarizes this argument in the following verses:
"God has not taken to Himself any son, nor is there any god with Him:
For then each god would have taken of that which he created and some
of them would have risen up over others." (23:91)
And Why, were there gods in earth and heaven other than God, they
(heaven and earth) would surely go to ruin." (21:22)


THE ONENESS OF GOD
The Quran reminds us of the falsity of all alleged gods. To the
worshippers of man-made objects, it asks:
"Do you worship what you have carved yourself?" (37:95) "Or have you
taken unto you others beside Him to be your protectors, even such as
have no power either for good or for harm to themselves?" (13:16)

To the worshippers of heavenly bodies it cites the story of Abraham:
"When night outspread over him he say a star and said, ‘This is my
Lord.’ But when it set he said, ‘I love not the setters.’ When he saw
the moon rising, he said, ‘This is my Lord.’ But when it set he said,
‘If my Lord does not guide me I shall surely be of the people gone
astray.’ When he say the sun rising, he said, ‘This is my Lord; this
is greater.’ But when it set he said, ‘O my people, surely I quit that
which you associate, I have turned my face to Him Who originated the
heavens and the earth; a man of pure faith, I am not of the
idolaters.’" (6:76-79)


THE BELIEVER’S ATTITUDE
In order to be a Muslim, i.e., to surrender oneself to God, it is
necessary to believe in the oneness of God, in the sense of His being
the only Creator, Preserver, Nourisher, etc. But this belief – later
on called "Tawhid Ar-Rububiyyah is not enough." Many of the idolaters
knew and believed that only the Supreme God could do all this. but
that was not enough to make them Muslims. To tawhid ar-rububiyyah one
must add tawhid al’uluhiyyah, i.e., one acknowledges the fact that is
God alone Who deserves to be worshipped, and thus abstains from
worshipping any other thing or being. Having achieved this knowledge
of the one true God, man should constantly have faith in Him, and
should allow nothing to induce him to deny truth. When faith enters a
person’s heart, it causes certain mental states which result in
certain actions. Taken together these mental states and actions are
the proof for the true faith. The Prophet said,
"Faith is that which resides firmly in the heart and which is proved
by deeds."

Foremost among those mental states is the feeling of gratitude towards
God, which could be said to be the essence of ‘ibada’ (worship). The
feeling of gratitude is so important that a non-believer is called
‘kafir,’ which means ‘one who denies a truth’ and also ‘one who is
ungrateful.’ A believer loves, and is grateful to God for the bounties
He bestowed upon him, but being aware of the fact that his good deeds,
whether mental or physical, are far from being commensurate with
Divine favors, he is always anxious lest God should punish him, here
or in the Hereafter. He, therefore, fears Him, surrenders himself to
Him and serves Him with great humility. One cannot be in such a mental
state without being almost all the time mindful of God. Remembering
God is thus the life force of faith, without which it fades and
withers away. The Quran tries to promote this feeling of gratitude by
repeating the attributes of God very frequently. We find most of these
attributes mentioned together in the following verses of the Quran:
"He is God; there is no god but He, He is the Knower of the unseen and
the visible; He is the All-Merciful, the All-Compassionate. He is God,
there is no God but He. He is the King, the All-Holy, the All-Peace,
the Guardian of Faith, the All-Preserver, the All-Mighty, the All-
Compeller, the All-Sublime. Glory be to God, above that they
associate! He is God the Creator, the Maker, the Shaper. To Him belong
the Names Most Beautiful. All that is in the heavens and the earth
magnifies Him; He is the All-Mighty, the All-Wise." (59:22-24)
"There is no god but He, the Living, the Everlasting. Slumber seizes
Him not, neither sleep; to Him belongs all that is in the heavens and
the earth. Who is there that shall intercede with Him save by His
leave? He knows what lies before them and what is after them, and they
comprehend not anything of His knowledge save such as He wills. His
throne comprises the heavens and earth; the preserving of them
oppresses Him not; He is the All-High, the All-Glorious." (2:255)
"People of the Book, go not beyond the bounds in your religion, and
say not as to God but the truth. The Messiah, Jesus son of Mary, was
only the Messenger of God, and His Word that He committed to Mary, and
a Spirit from Him. So believe in God and His Messengers, and say not,
‘Three.’ Refrain; better is it for you. God is only one God. Glory be
to Him – (He is) above having a son." (4:171)

—————————————
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Runtime crash in z/OS

_(thread depth: 4)_

- **From:** docdwarf@panix.com ()
- **Date:** 2012-01-26T17:09:28+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<jfs1c8$g1m$1@reader1.panix.com>`
- **References:** `<ji31i7lna0q0jas6bgoh8grdgp6kjtiel4@4ax.com> <b56516fc-4ff8-463f-aa6e-7e8b3c387976@vh10g2000pbc.googlegroups.com> <tha1i79dl71lketdjl6kv1mb9d2oocsp3s@4ax.com> <lawrence.greenwald-B42BF9.19333325012012@5ad64b5e.bb.sky.com>`

```
In article <lawrence.greenwald-B42BF9.19333325012012@5ad64b5e.bb.sky.com>,
Lawrence Greenwald  <lawrence.greenwald@sbcglobal.net> wrote:

[snip]

>That's why I never directly manipulate the FD area.

You're not the only one, Mr Greenwald.  A few years back someone posted in 
this forum 
<http://groups.google.com/group/comp.lang.cobol/msg/fee1c188d1805333?dmode=source> 
and asked:

--begin quoted text:

So... I was being consulted on a program's design and mention was made of 
the FD; reflexively I responded that the record layout in the FD should be 
as simple as possible, e.g.

 FD  INFILE
     BLOCK CONTAINS 0 RECORDS
                            .
 01  INFILE-REC        PIC X(160).

... and that everything should be dealt with in WORKING-STORAGE using READ 
INTO and WRITE FROM.

This, of course, is what was taught to me lo, those many decades back; the 
specific of 'Thou shalt not perform arithmetic manipulations in file 
buffers' was expanded into the zero-tolerance rule of 'Thou shalt not 
touch file buffers; all will be done in WORKING-STORAGE via READ INTO and 
WRITE FROM.'

--end quoted text

DD
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Runtime crash in z/OS

_(thread depth: 4)_

- **From:** "Vince Coen" <VBCoen@gmail.com>
- **Date:** 2012-01-26T21:08:59
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1327612139@f1.n250.z2.fidonet.ftn>`
- **References:** `<ji31i7lna0q0jas6bgoh8grdgp6kjtiel4@4ax.com> <b56516fc-4ff8-463f-aa6e-7e8b3c387976@vh10g2000pbc.googlegroups.com> <tha1i79dl71lketdjl6kv1mb9d2oocsp3s@4ax.com> <lawrence.greenwald-B42BF9.19333325012012@5ad64b5e.bb.sky.com>`

```
Hello docdwarf!

26 Jan 12 17:09, you wrote to All:

 > You're not the only one, Mr Greenwald.  A few years back someone
 > posted in this forum
 >  <http://groups.google.com/group/comp.lang.cobol/msg/fee1c188d1805333?
 > dmode=source> and asked:

 > --begin quoted text:

 > So... I was being consulted on a program's design and mention was made
 > of the FD; reflexively I responded that the record layout in the FD
 > should be as simple as possible, e.g.

 >  FD  INFILE
 >      BLOCK CONTAINS 0 RECORDS
 >                             .
 >  01  INFILE-REC        PIC X(160).

 > .... and that everything should be dealt with in WORKING-STORAGE using
 > READ INTO and WRITE FROM.

 > This, of course, is what was taught to me lo, those many decades back;
 > the specific of 'Thou shalt not perform arithmetic manipulations in
 > file buffers' was expanded into the zero-tolerance rule of 'Thou shalt
 > not touch file buffers; all will be done in WORKING-STORAGE via READ
 > INTO and WRITE FROM.'

 > --end quoted text

There was a good reason for this methology, why I hear you ask?

On some of the earlier compilers going back to IBM 1401, 360/30 (ish) etc:
1.  If the file was closed for whatever reason the data within the FD would
be junk, you would not get a regular exception error (suprise, suprise).
2.  Some compilers subject to what the compiler configuration settings
where you could even get an Abend (for those that don't, it was very
fatal).
3.  Also on 360's code generated was longer both in length and time to
execute using FD's as they used a buffer system that was in high memory.
4.  WS used low memory on both (and including ICL 1500,1900,2900) so
programs where a lot more efficient and did not break so much outside of a
programmers control.

There were also other procedures to adopt to keep the code as tight as
possible including avoiding Compute verbs where at all poss, eg, stick to
the regular four, add, subtract, multiply and divide. Also Perform's across
bounderies and a ton of other stuff.

I recall creating programs that went through all of the coding
structures and looking at the assembler produced (compiler can be told to
list it immediately after the Cobol statement) to find best practice for a
given version of a compiler.

Heck, have not thought about that in years but.. still do slightly similar
with compilers for PC's but not so important these days as Ram
availability is somewhat larger than 16kw - 256kw.


Don't mind me, just an almost 65 year old remembering back to the early
1960's  :)


Vince
```

```
docdwarf@panix.com wrote:
> In article <1327612139@f1.n250.z2.fidonet.ftn>,
> Vince Coen <VBCoen@gmail.com> wrote:
…[86 more quoted lines elided]…
> DD

A pleasant walk down memory lane.

I too had all of these thngs instilled in me in the late sixties working on 
sytem 360s and 1401s.

But once I became a Journeyman  Programmer and started thinking for myself , 
I re-examining the Holy Writ I had been given, (I was young and 
agnostic...).  I realised that these things are like the Pirate's Code: 
Guidelines, rather than immutable Laws that must be obeyed always.

I found it was perfectly OK to manipulate data in IO buffers (provided the 
file was open and addressibility to said buffer was established.) Doing so 
saved storage which was very expensive at that time and we had little of it.

Perhaps I can entertain Doc as he suggested... a scenario where READ INTO 
and WRITE FROM could be less than advantageous, even harmful.

Consider a file with millions of records on it.

Every time you read one of these records you are moving it to somewhere 
else, and every time you write it you are moving it from somewhere else.

Unnecessarily. You don't HAVE to do it.

Even if the records are no more than say, 1K in size, you will have MVCed 
billions (probably trillions...) of characters. No big deal; that's what 
computers are for... But these are SS instructions and before the extended 
instruction set was added with the advent of System 370, the maximum length 
you could move in a single instruction was 255 bytes... and it was one of 
the longest instructions in terms of time, in the instruction set.

So, because your overnight run now takes a few minutes more than it needed 
to, the Batch window closes before the Payroll can be processed and everyone 
has to wait for their pay check.

But little Johnny needs an operation and the Hospital is waiting for his 
Mother to show she can pay for it. She told them she would have the money 
Tuesday morning, but she doesn't because the Payroll didn't run. 
Frantically, she throws herself upon their mercy and pleads she will have 
the cash on Wednesday. But her credibility is gone and, without the right 
box ticked by Admin, the consulting surgeon cannot proceed, so he resumes 
his golf game.

Johnny dies.

His mother, blinded by grief has a mental breakdown, and finally, one night, 
she takes an axe and goes looking for the guy who caused the payroll not to 
run... Arriving at Doc's place around midnight, she is surprised to find a 
number of other people who also had their lives destroyed lurking in the 
shrubbery waiting for an opportunity...  They pass the time deciding various 
horrific ways in which to despatch our favourite Dwarf until the morning 
comes  and the front door opens. Out comes the Doc, with a song in his heart 
and a "Hi Ho, Hi Ho, it's off to work I go", intent on getting into the 
office and moving some more bytes around.

He never makes it.

Pete.
```

```
I am relying purely on memory here and it was a long time ago.

I seem to recall someone explaining that the new INSPECT statement could 
utilize the TRANSFORM and TRANSLATE (table driven) instructions of the S360 
instruction set. So, it seems it was driven (at least as far as IBM was 
concerned, and they had considerable leverage on things like standards at 
the time) by the desire to  better utilize the existing architecture and 
provide COBOL with access to instructions that could do more.

porous, but I'm pretty sure that was it.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Why did they replace EXAMINE with INSPECT was Re: Runtime crash in z/OS

_(thread depth: 10)_

- **From:** Rick Smith <rs847925@gmail.com>
- **Date:** 2012-01-29T02:33:22-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<c41c84b5-5bb8-4655-9480-aacd5628b4af@x19g2000yqh.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<ji31i7lna0q0jas6bgoh8grdgp6kjtiel4@4ax.com> <lawrence.greenwald-B42BF9.19333325012012@5ad64b5e.bb.sky.com> <1327612139@f1.n250.z2.fidonet.ftn> <jft3cs$e1i$2@reader1.panix.com> <9offj3F7ujU1@mid.individual.net> <d3957575-2df3-4732-bcdf-bf44ae339b90@c20g2000vbb.googlegroups.com> <9ojb4lFekuU1@mid.individual.net> <1qa9i790t24l74k1hd4qu7sdbdv4nta4cn@4ax.com>`

```
On Jan 28, 9:17 pm, Clark F Morris <cfmpub...@ns.sympatico.ca> wrote:

[snip]

> I was not impressed with several things they did with 1985 standard
> including the ambiguous END-ADD instead of END-ON or END-SIZE-ERROR,
> END-READ instead of END-AT or END-END (but not END-PERFORM) but the
> one that seemed ludicrous to me with replacement of EXAMINE with
> INSPECT so an explanation for that would be appreciated.

Scope terminators terminate the scope of STATEMENTS not PHRASES.
ADD is a statement--ON is a phrase.

CODASYL 1969 proposed "The deletion of the EXAMINE statement and
the inclusion of a more powerful statement, INSPECT, in its
place." CODASYL 1976 proposed "An enhancement to the INSPECT
statement to simplify data transformation."

These references to history don't explain why, but they do
suggest that "more powerful" and "simplify" were factors.
```

```
On Sun, 29 Jan 2012 02:33:22 -0800 (PST), Rick Smith
<rs847925@gmail.com> wrote:

>On Jan 28, 9:17ï¿½pm, Clark F Morris <cfmpub...@ns.sympatico.ca> wrote:
>
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>ADD is a statement--ON is a phrase.

I vehemently contend that ON SIZE ERROR could be better written as IF
SIZE ERROR and AT END better written as IF END.  The scope should end
a Conditional statement, not an imperative one.  The ADD, READ, etc.
all execute unconditionally.  Consider the Ambiguity of the following.

ADD A TO B
  ON SIZE ERROR
     ADD 1 TO B-OVERFLOW-COUNTER
END-ADD

In this case the END-ADD terminates the first ADD statement not the
second.  

>
>CODASYL 1969 proposed "The deletion of the EXAMINE statement and
>the inclusion of a more powerful statement, INSPECT, in its
>place." CODASYL 1976 proposed "An enhancement to the INSPECT
>statement to simplify data transformation."

Since the upgrade was to replace EXAMINE with INSPECT why not just add
the TRANSFORM and TRANSLATE capabilities to EXAMINE.  In English
EXAMINE and INSPECT can be treated as synonyms.

Clark Morris
>
>These references to history don't explain why, but they do
>suggest that "more powerful" and "simplify" were factors.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Why did they replace EXAMINE with INSPECT was Re: Runtime crash in z/OS

_(thread depth: 12)_

- **From:** Rick Smith <rs847925@gmail.com>
- **Date:** 2012-01-29T15:58:03-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<c819832d-2fc8-49f5-803b-1dfe58cf7706@k10g2000yqk.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<ji31i7lna0q0jas6bgoh8grdgp6kjtiel4@4ax.com> <lawrence.greenwald-B42BF9.19333325012012@5ad64b5e.bb.sky.com> <1327612139@f1.n250.z2.fidonet.ftn> <jft3cs$e1i$2@reader1.panix.com> <9offj3F7ujU1@mid.individual.net> <d3957575-2df3-4732-bcdf-bf44ae339b90@c20g2000vbb.googlegroups.com> <9ojb4lFekuU1@mid.individual.net> <1qa9i790t24l74k1hd4qu7sdbdv4nta4cn@4ax.com> <c41c84b5-5bb8-4655-9480-aacd5628b4af@x19g2000yqh.googlegroups.com> <fg1bi7d8ekq3psqeiajdgh3h3b7ndp07l8@4ax.com>`

```
On Jan 29, 4:38 pm, Clark F Morris <cfmpub...@ns.sympatico.ca> wrote:
> On Sun, 29 Jan 2012 02:33:22 -0800 (PST), Rick Smith
>
…[25 more quoted lines elided]…
> second.

I recall enough discussions of this issue that I choose
to NOT reignite that fire. g>

>
>
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> EXAMINE and INSPECT can be treated as synonyms.

First the timing:
COBOL 68 - EXAMINE ... TALLYING or REPLACING
COBOL 74 - INSPECT ... TALLYING or REPLACING or TALLYING ... REPLACING
COBOL 85 - INSPECT ... CONVERTING (added)

Having replaced EXAMINE with the "more powerful" INSPECT in 74,
there was no EXAMINE in 85 to which to add the capabilities of
TRANSFORM.

I still can't say why the change was made, but I like the
current statements.
```

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<2c9661d3-788e-4cc0-83d1-3a87863c1af7@ra5g2000pbc.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<ji31i7lna0q0jas6bgoh8grdgp6kjtiel4@4ax.com> <lawrence.greenwald-B42BF9.19333325012012@5ad64b5e.bb.sky.com> <1327612139@f1.n250.z2.fidonet.ftn> <jft3cs$e1i$2@reader1.panix.com> <9offj3F7ujU1@mid.individual.net> <d3957575-2df3-4732-bcdf-bf44ae339b90@c20g2000vbb.googlegroups.com> <9ojb4lFekuU1@mid.individual.net> <1qa9i790t24l74k1hd4qu7sdbdv4nta4cn@4ax.com> <c41c84b5-5bb8-4655-9480-aacd5628b4af@x19g2000yqh.googlegroups.com> <fg1bi7d8ekq3psqeiajdgh3h3b7ndp07l8@4ax.com>`

```
On Jan 30, 10:38 am, Clark F Morris <cfmpub...@ns.sympatico.ca> wrote:
> On Sun, 29 Jan 2012 02:33:22 -0800 (PST), Rick Smith
>
…[25 more quoted lines elided]…
> second.

The second ADD is an imperative statement. Imperative statements do
not have a scope and thus do not allow a scope terminator.

The first ADD is a conditional statement, made so by the ON SIZE
ERROR, and thus has a scope. The scope requires termination either by
a scope terminator or a sentence terminator (full stop).

There is no ambiguity.

> The ADD, READ, etc. all execute unconditionally.

No. Consider:

    READ x AT END dosomething END-READ

The READ (ie a transfer of a record from file to the record buffer)
occurs conditionally, if there is an 'AT END' condition then there is
no transfer to the record buffer, ie no READ is done.

It is the READ that is conditional, the AT END is a condition.



> >CODASYL 1969 proposed "The deletion of the EXAMINE statement and
> >the inclusion of a more powerful statement, INSPECT, in its
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> EXAMINE and INSPECT can be treated as synonyms.
```

```
On Mon, 30 Jan 2012 13:30:44 -0500, Kerry Liles
<"kerry.liles[minus_the_spam]"@gmail.com> wrote:

>On 1/30/2012 1:12 PM, Richard wrote:
>> On Jan 30, 10:38 am, Clark F Morris<cfmpub...@ns.sympatico.ca>  wrote:
…[41 more quoted lines elided]…
>I am afraid I do not see the difference in the two ADD statements.


It follows the behavior of any ADD statement that overflows without an
ON SIZE ERROR clause.  Usually truncation (for PIC 999 599+600=199),
some odd result (common for fields physically capable of holding a
bigger range than specified by the PIC, and "standard" truncation is
not enabled - common on binary fields, for example), or an exception,
depending on language version, compiler options, data types, etc...
```
