Nomen Nescio wrote:
>>>>> Consider a file with millions of records on it.
>>>>>
…[41 more quoted lines elided]…
> him a NET question though ;-)

Thanks for the supportive and light hearted mail. :-)

It's a bit ironic because I am currently writing a very large Windows Script 
for a very complicated install of the PRIMA Migration Toolset. Windows 
Scripting (WSF or WSH files that contain VBScript or JavaScript, sometimes 
WMI as well) is the Windows platform equivalent of mainframe JCL. But these 
scripts are much more powerful. It isn't JUST about allocating datasets, 
defining jobs and job steps, and checking return codes (although the 
equivalent of those actions are all part of it). It is about installing 
components and getting them registered in both Native Code and as .Net 
Assemblies. Decisions have to be taken (in Script) as to what is already 
present, what isn't, and whether adding what isn't will upset what is, then 
checking whether the install was successful and if it wasn't what can be 
done about it... :-)

It's like JCL on steroids... :-)

This particular script is aimed at a particular platform so it has to 
install things which would very often be already present. The code is 
already approaching 1000 lines (I try to debug it 20 lines at a time...) and 
is more of a program than JCL...:-)

Don't ask me anything about .Net; I'm too busy at the moment :-)

Pete.
```

```
Lawrence Greenwald wrote:
> In article <9ojb4lFekuU1@mid.individual.net>,
> "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[22 more quoted lines elided]…
> I/O operation was precious.

Ah, THAT's where it is... I knew it was around here somewhere...

And that would be as opposed to today, when IO operations are NOT precious? 
Ever been billed for mainframe use and noticed that the formula is based on 
EXCPs (Execute Channel Program macros..."IO"s to the laity), as well as CPU 
usage? No?  That's what comes of not charging programmers for wasting 
resources; they don't care...


>
> Now we got buffering, blocked files, VSAM, etc.

Gosh, really?   We had to implement double buffering manually in the 60s. 
And then COBOL came along with its ALTERNATE AREA and took all the fun out 
of it. And why they replaced that lovely ISAM and BDAM architecture with 
VSAM, I'll never understand. It was loads of fun developing our own hashing 
algorithms for BDAM and re-organizing second level overflow on ISAM; REAL 
programmers don't need automatic Balanced Tree free space 
management...Control Interval and Control Area splitting... all unnecessary 
frippery, we were fine with what we had.

Why, I knew a guy once who spent 3 days going through a dump of the 
microcode in the newly available IBM 3330 disk drives just so he could find 
out how Rotational Position Sensing worked with a Block MUX. Then, when he 
had it sussed, he tried to improve the sorting of seek addresses so it would 
work faster. All just to try and improve IO... Not a bit of need for it, 
just showing off. (To be fair he WAS working for CDC and they WERE selling 
"plug compatible" 3330 drives against IBM so there WAS some competition 
driving his effort. The result was an order from the largest IBM user in the 
Southern Hemisphere at that time for millions of dollars worth of CDC 
drives.)




>In my shop,

You have a shop?! Wow! I had a shop once; it sold ice cream and water melons 
on hot days at the beach. (Sorry for mid-sentence interruption, but I really 
couldn't resist...)

> we regularly do backups on a million record (1.3 million records as of
> about now) VSAM KSDS file that each have 1,100 bytes in them. Takes
> less than 10 minutes real time.

Hmmm... haven't managed to make the jump into a modern Relational Databse 
yet? I can help you with that.

I have a shop...

(http://primacomputing.co.nz) (very handy for getting you unlocked from 
those VSAM files...Mind you, we don't convert ESDS or RRDS although we 
probably could if you paid us enough... (Sorry, that was a the shopkeeper in 
me coming out...lived too long in England.)

>
> We have batch processing every Friday night starting at around 6:00
> PM. We're usually done by 9:30 PM. On the same Friday night.

And miss "The Big Bang Theory" on TV... I don't think so...

The online system can't hack it, then? Have to finish the updates in batch, 
and then there is all those pesky reports, accumulations,  and control 
breaks that people want, not to mention the files that must be batch 
synchronized across systems ... It must be just like living in the 1980s...
Do you print on green lineflo?

>
> I'm big on efficiency and take advantage of any JCL options that speed
> up operations.

You mean like using IEBGENER? or GDGs that reset themselves automatically? 
So what about ROSPROCS or REXX? Too cutting edge? Quite right... SPF and 
SPUFI does it for me.
>
> Are you?

Nah, I don't understand all that efficiency stuff... leave it to the geeks. 
I stick to what I know... (ADD 1 TO COUNT...)

I like a computer I can take with me; that Big Iron stuff is far too 
complicated and mysterious.

Besides, efficiency of execution is way overrated. Ask any COBOL guy and 
he'll tell you clarity of source code is what matters.

"When you're called out of bed at 3 in the morning..."

Have you visited this forum before? :-)

Pete.

L�ko Willms wrote:
> Am 27.01.2012 12:20, schrieb Pete Dashwood:
>
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> barbarism prevails!

Please Lueko, calm yourself. It is whimsy, not fact. :-)

You are absolutely right that such a society (if it existed) would not be a 
place where civilized people might want to live. And as long as people like 
yourself are outraged by the idea of it, there is less chance of such a 
society evolving.

I don't honestly believe that someone (in most civilized countries) who 
could not afford a life saving procedure would be allowed to die because of 
that. (Wouldn't you lie to them and SAY you could pay or wave a credit card 
at them or something...?)

Unfortunately, we cannot say the same about NON Life Threatening operations 
and many people have to suffer because they cannot afford treatment.

You probably already know that Health Care in Germany is some of the finest 
in the World, but it is also one of the most expensive places. I remember my 
Krankenversicherung was equivalent to around  $US 2000 per year. In the UK 
(BUPA) it was around $US 800, and in New Zealand it is around $US 700. (Most 
people here don't have cover because the Public Health system (although much 
maligned) is actually quite good.)

Don't be alarmed; my piece was tongue-in-cheek; Johhny didn't die and the 
surgeon was a lousy golfer. Doc Dwarf is alive and well, and...

They All Lived Happily Ever After...

(Now, back to work...) :-)

Pete
